   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Equipartition theorem
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div class="noprint" id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
     Jump to:
     <a href="#mw-head">
      navigation
     </a>
     ,
     <a href="#p-search">
      search
     </a>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="mw-parser-output">

          </a>
         </div>
        </td>

      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thermally_Agitated_Molecule.gif">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="280" data-file-width="280" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Thermally_Agitated_Molecule.gif/220px-Thermally_Agitated_Molecule.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/23/Thermally_Agitated_Molecule.gif 1.5x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thermally_Agitated_Molecule.gif" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <b>
          Thermal motion
         </b>
         of an
         <a href="/wiki/Alpha_helix" title="Alpha helix">
          α-helical
         </a>
         <a href="/wiki/Peptide" title="Peptide">
          peptide
         </a>
         . The jittery motion is random and complex, and the energy of any particular atom can fluctuate wildly. Nevertheless, the equipartition theorem allows the
         <i>
          average
         </i>
         <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">
          kinetic energy
         </a>
         of each atom to be computed, as well as the average potential energies of many vibrational modes. The grey, red and blue spheres represent
         <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">
          atoms
         </a>
         of
         <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">
          carbon
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">
          oxygen
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">
          nitrogen
         </a>
         , respectively; the smaller white spheres represent atoms of
         <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">
          hydrogen
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Classical_physics" title="Classical physics">
        classical
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Statistical_mechanics" title="Statistical mechanics">
        statistical mechanics
       </a>
       , the
       <b>
        equipartition theorem
       </b>
       relates the
       <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">
        temperature
       </a>
       of a system to its average
       <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">
        energies
       </a>
       . The equipartition theorem is also known as the
       <b>
        law of equipartition
       </b>
       ,
       <b>
        equipartition of energy
       </b>
       , or simply
       <b>
        equipartition
       </b>
       . The original idea of equipartition was that, in
       <a href="/wiki/Thermal_equilibrium" title="Thermal equilibrium">
        thermal equilibrium
       </a>
       , energy is shared equally among all of its various forms; for example, the average
       <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">
        kinetic energy
       </a>
       per
       <a href="/wiki/Degrees_of_freedom_(physics_and_chemistry)" title="Degrees of freedom (physics and chemistry)">
        degree of freedom
       </a>
       in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Translation_(physics)" title="Translation (physics)">
        translational motion
       </a>
       of a molecule should equal that in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rotational_motion" title="Rotational motion">
        rotational motion
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The equipartition theorem makes quantitative predictions. Like the
       <a href="/wiki/Virial_theorem" title="Virial theorem">
        virial theorem
       </a>
       , it gives the total average kinetic and potential energies for a system at a given temperature, from which the system's
       <a href="/wiki/Heat_capacity" title="Heat capacity">
        heat capacity
       </a>
       can be computed. However, equipartition also gives the average values of individual components of the energy, such as the kinetic energy of a particular particle or the potential energy of a single
       <a href="/wiki/Spring_(device)" title="Spring (device)">
        spring
       </a>
       . For example, it predicts that every atom in a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monatomic" title="Monatomic">
        monatomic
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Ideal_gas" title="Ideal gas">
        ideal gas
       </a>
       has an average kinetic energy of (3/2)
       <i>
        k
       </i>
       <sub>
        B
       </sub>
       <i>
        T
       </i>
       in thermal equilibrium, where
       <i>
        k
       </i>
       <sub>
        B
       </sub>
       is the
       <a href="/wiki/Boltzmann_constant" title="Boltzmann constant">
        Boltzmann constant
       </a>
       and
       <i>
        T
       </i>
       is the
       <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_temperature" title="Thermodynamic temperature">
        (thermodynamic) temperature
       </a>
       . More generally, equipartition can be applied to any
       <a href="/wiki/Classical_physics" title="Classical physics">
        classical system
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Thermal_equilibrium" title="Thermal equilibrium">
        thermal equilibrium
       </a>
       , no matter how complicated. It can be used to derive the
       <a href="/wiki/Ideal_gas_law" title="Ideal gas law">
        ideal gas law
       </a>
       , and the
       <a href="/wiki/Dulong%E2%80%93Petit_law" title="Dulong–Petit law">
        Dulong–Petit law
       </a>
       for the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Specific_heat_capacity" title="Specific heat capacity">
        specific heat capacities
       </a>
       of solids. The equipartition theorem can also be used to predict the properties of
       <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">
        stars
       </a>
       , even
       <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">
        white dwarfs
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">
        neutron stars
       </a>
       , since it holds even when
       <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">
        relativistic
       </a>
       effects are considered.
      </p>
      <p>
       Although the equipartition theorem makes accurate predictions in certain conditions, it is inaccurate when
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quantum_physics" title="Quantum physics">
        quantum effects
       </a>
       are significant, such as at low temperatures. When the
       <a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy" title="Thermal energy">
        thermal energy
       </a>
       <i>
        k
       </i>
       <sub>
        B
       </sub>
       <i>
        T
       </i>
       is smaller than the quantum energy spacing in a particular
       <a href="/wiki/Degrees_of_freedom_(physics_and_chemistry)" title="Degrees of freedom (physics and chemistry)">
        degree of freedom
       </a>
       , the average energy and heat capacity of this degree of freedom are less than the values predicted by equipartition. Such a degree of freedom is said to be "frozen out" when the thermal energy is much smaller than this spacing. For example, the heat capacity of a solid decreases at low temperatures as various types of motion become frozen out, rather than remaining constant as predicted by equipartition. Such decreases in heat capacity were among the first signs to physicists of the 19th century that classical physics was incorrect and that a new, more subtle, scientific model was required. Along with other evidence, equipartition's failure to model
       <a href="/wiki/Black-body_radiation" title="Black-body radiation">
        black-body radiation
       </a>
       —also known as the
       <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet_catastrophe" title="Ultraviolet catastrophe">
        ultraviolet catastrophe
       </a>
       —led
       <a href="/wiki/Max_Planck" title="Max Planck">
        Max Planck
       </a>
       to suggest that energy in the oscillators in an object, which emit light, were quantized, a revolutionary hypothesis that spurred the development of
       <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">
        quantum mechanics
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory" title="Quantum field theory">
        quantum field theory
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

             <a href="#The_microcanonical_ensemble">
              <span class="tocnumber">
               5.3.2
              </span>
              <span class="toctext">
               The microcanonical ensemble
              </span>
             </a>
            </li>
           </ul>
          </li>
         </ul>
        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25">
         <a href="#Limitations">
          <span class="tocnumber">
           6
          </span>
          <span class="toctext">
           Limitations
          </span>
         </a>
         <ul>
          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-26">

             Failure due to quantum effects
            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
         </ul>
        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28">
         <a href="#See_also">
          <span class="tocnumber">
           7
          </span>
          <span class="toctext">

          </span>

        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29">

          </span>

        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30">

          </span>

      </div>
      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Basic_concept_and_simple_examples">
        Basic concept and simple examples
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Equipartition_theorem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Basic concept and simple examples">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">
        Kinetic energy
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Heat_capacity" title="Heat capacity">
        Heat capacity
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MaxwellBoltzmann-en.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="463" data-file-width="700" height="291" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/MaxwellBoltzmann-en.svg/440px-MaxwellBoltzmann-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/MaxwellBoltzmann-en.svg/660px-MaxwellBoltzmann-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/MaxwellBoltzmann-en.svg/880px-MaxwellBoltzmann-en.svg.png 2x" width="440"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MaxwellBoltzmann-en.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Figure 2. Probability density functions of the molecular speed for four
         <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">
          noble gases
         </a>
         at a
         <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">
          temperature
         </a>
         of 298.15
         <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">
          K
         </a>
         (25
         <a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">
          °C
         </a>
         ). The four gases are
         <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">
          helium
         </a>
         (
         <sup>
          4
         </sup>
         He),
         <a href="/wiki/Neon" title="Neon">
          neon
         </a>
         (
         <sup>
          20
         </sup>
         Ne),
         <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">
          argon
         </a>
         (
         <sup>
          40
         </sup>
         Ar) and
         <a href="/wiki/Xenon" title="Xenon">
          xenon
         </a>
         (
         <sup>
          132
         </sup>
         Xe); the superscripts indicate their
         <a href="/wiki/Mass_number" title="Mass number">
          mass numbers
         </a>
         . These probability density functions have
         <a href="/wiki/Dimensional_analysis" title="Dimensional analysis">
          dimensions
         </a>
         of probability times inverse speed; since probability is dimensionless, they can be expressed in units of seconds per meter.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The name "equipartition" means "equal division," as derived from the
       <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
        Latin
       </a>
       <i>
        equi
       </i>
       from the antecedent, æquus ("equal or even"), and partition from the noun,
       <i>
        partitio
       </i>
       ("division, portion").
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The original concept of equipartition was that the total
       <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">
        kinetic energy
       </a>
       of a system is shared equally among all of its independent parts,
       <i>
        on the average
       </i>
       , once the system has reached thermal equilibrium. Equipartition also makes quantitative predictions for these energies. For example, it predicts that every atom of an inert
       <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">
        noble gas
       </a>
       , in thermal equilibrium at temperature
       <i>
        T
       </i>
       , has an average translational kinetic energy of (3/2)
       <i>
        k
       </i>
       <sub>
        B
       </sub>
       <i>
        T
       </i>
       , where
       <i>
        k
       </i>
       <sub>
        B
       </sub>
       is the
       <a href="/wiki/Boltzmann_constant" title="Boltzmann constant">
        Boltzmann constant
       </a>
       . As a consequence, since kinetic energy is equal to 1/2(mass)(velocity)
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       , the heavier atoms of
       <a href="/wiki/Xenon" title="Xenon">
        xenon
       </a>
       have a lower average speed than do the lighter atoms of
       <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">
        helium
       </a>
       at the same temperature. Figure 2 shows the
       <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%E2%80%93Boltzmann_distribution" title="Maxwell–Boltzmann distribution">
        Maxwell–Boltzmann distribution
       </a>
       for the speeds of the atoms in four noble gases.
      </p>
      <p>
       In this example, the key point is that the kinetic energy is quadratic in the velocity. The equipartition theorem shows that in thermal equilibrium, any
       <a href="/wiki/Degrees_of_freedom_(physics_and_chemistry)" title="Degrees of freedom (physics and chemistry)">
        degree of freedom
       </a>
       (such as a component of the position or velocity of a particle) which appears only quadratically in the energy has an average energy of ​
       <span class="frac nowrap">
        <sup>
         1
        </sup>
        ⁄
        <sub>
         2
        </sub>
       </span>
       <i>
        k
       </i>
       <sub>
        B
       </sub>
       <i>
        T
       </i>
       and therefore contributes ​
       <span class="frac nowrap">
        <sup>
         1
        </sup>
        ⁄
        <sub>
         2
        </sub>
       </span>
       <i>
        k
       </i>
       <sub>
        B
       </sub>
       to the system's
       <a href="/wiki/Heat_capacity" title="Heat capacity">
        heat capacity
       </a>
       . This has many applications.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Translational_energy_and_ideal_gases">
        Translational energy and ideal gases
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Equipartition_theorem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Translational energy and ideal gases">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Ideal_gas" title="Ideal gas">
        Ideal gas
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The (Newtonian) kinetic energy of a particle of mass
       <i>
        m
       </i>
       , velocity
       <b>
        v
       </b>
       is given by
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle H_{\text{kin}}={\tfrac {1}{2}}m|\mathbf {v} |^{2}={\tfrac {1}{2}}m\left(v_{x}^{2}+v_{y}^{2}+v_{z}^{2}\right),}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <msub>
               <mi>
                H
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mtext>
                 kin
                </mtext>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
                <mfrac>
                 <mn>
                  1
                 </mn>
                 <mn>
                  2
                 </mn>
                </mfrac>
               </mstyle>
              </mrow>
              <mi>
               m
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mo stretchy="false">
                |
               </mo>
              </mrow>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                v
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <msup>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mo stretchy="false">
                 |
                </mo>
               </mrow>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mn>
                 2
                </mn>
               </mrow>
              </msup>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
                <mfrac>
                 <mn>
                  1
                 </mn>
                 <mn>
                  2
                 </mn>
                </mfrac>
               </mstyle>
              </mrow>
              <mi>
               m
              </mi>
              <mrow>
               <mo>
                (
               </mo>
               <mrow>
                <msubsup>
                 <mi>
                  v
                 </mi>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mi>
                   x
                  </mi>
                 </mrow>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mn>
                   2
                  </mn>
                 </mrow>
                </msubsup>
                <mo>
                 +
                </mo>
                <msubsup>
                 <mi>
                  v
                 </mi>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mi>
                   y
                  </mi>
                 </mrow>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mn>
                   2
                  </mn>
                 </mrow>
                </msubsup>
                <mo>
                 +
                </mo>
                <msubsup>
                 <mi>
                  v
                 </mi>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mi>
                   z
                  </mi>
                 </mrow>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mn>
                   2
                  </mn>
                 </mrow>
                </msubsup>
               </mrow>
               <mo>
                )
               </mo>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ,
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle H_{\text{kin}}={\tfrac {1}{2}}m|\mathbf {v} |^{2}={\tfrac {1}{2}}m\left(v_{x}^{2}+v_{y}^{2}+v_{z}^{2}\right),}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="H_{{{\text{kin}}}}={\tfrac  12}m|{\mathbf  {v}}|^{2}={\tfrac  {1}{2}}m\left(v_{x}^{2}+v_{y}^{2}+v_{z}^{2}\right)," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1e54ae39b34cd3c9fbd0e317c67522b367bb651c" style="vertical-align: -1.171ex; width:37.651ex; height:3.676ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where
       <i>
        v
        <sub>
         x
        </sub>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        v
        <sub>
         y
        </sub>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        v
        <sub>
         z
        </sub>
       </i>
       are the Cartesian components of the velocity
       <b>
        v
       </b>
       . Here,
       <i>
        H
       </i>
       is short for
       <a href="/wiki/Hamiltonian_(quantum_mechanics)" title="Hamiltonian (quantum mechanics)">
        Hamiltonian
       </a>
       , and used henceforth as a symbol for energy because the
       <a href="/wiki/Hamiltonian_mechanics" title="Hamiltonian mechanics">
        Hamiltonian formalism
       </a>
       plays a central role in the most
       <a href="#General_formulation_of_the_equipartition_theorem">
        general form
       </a>
       of the equipartition theorem.
      </p>
      <p>
       Since the kinetic energy is quadratic in the components of the velocity, by equipartition these three components each contribute ​
       <span class="frac nowrap">
        <sup>
         1
        </sup>
        ⁄
        <sub>
         2
        </sub>
       </span>
       <i>
        k
       </i>
       <sub>
        B
       </sub>
       <i>
        T
       </i>
       to the average kinetic energy in thermal equilibrium. Thus the average kinetic energy of the particle is (3/2)
       <i>
        k
       </i>
       <sub>
        B
       </sub>
       <i>
        T
       </i>
       , as in the example of noble gases above.
      </p>
      <p>
       More generally, in an ideal gas, the total energy consists purely of (translational) kinetic energy: by assumption, the particles have no internal degrees of freedom and move independently of one another. Equipartition therefore predicts that the average total energy of an ideal gas of
       <i>
        N
       </i>
       particles is (3/2)
       <i>
        N k
       </i>
       <sub>
        B
       </sub>
       <i>
        T
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       It follows that the
       <a href="/wiki/Heat_capacity" title="Heat capacity">
        heat capacity
       </a>
       of the gas is (3/2)
       <i>
        N k
       </i>
       <sub>
        B
       </sub>
       and hence, in particular, the heat capacity of a
       <a href="/wiki/Mole_(unit)" title="Mole (unit)">
        mole
       </a>
       of such gas particles is (3/2)
       <i>
        N
       </i>
       <sub>
        A
       </sub>
       <i>
        k
       </i>
       <sub>
        B
       </sub>
       = (3/2)
       <i>
        R
       </i>
       , where
       <i>
        N
       </i>
       <sub>
        A
       </sub>
       is the
       <a href="/wiki/Avogadro_constant" title="Avogadro constant">
        Avogadro constant
       </a>
       and
       <i>
        R
       </i>
       is the
       <a href="/wiki/Gas_constant" title="Gas constant">
        gas constant
       </a>
       . Since
       <i>
        R
       </i>
       ≈ 2
       <a href="/wiki/Calorie" title="Calorie">
        cal
       </a>
       /(
       <a href="/wiki/Mole_(unit)" title="Mole (unit)">
        mol
       </a>
       ·
       <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">
        K
       </a>
       ), equipartition predicts that the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molar_heat_capacity" title="Molar heat capacity">
        molar heat capacity
       </a>
       of an ideal gas is roughly 3 cal/(mol·K). This prediction is confirmed by experiment.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kundt_1876_3-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-kundt_1876-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The mean kinetic energy also allows the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Root_mean_square_speed" title="Root mean square speed">
        root mean square speed
       </a>
       <i>
        v
       </i>
       <sub>
        rms
       </sub>
       of the gas particles to be calculated:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle v_{\text{rms}}={\sqrt {\langle v^{2}\rangle }}={\sqrt {\frac {3k_{B}T}{m}}}={\sqrt {\frac {3RT}{M}}},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <msub>
               <mi>
                v
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mtext>
                 rms
                </mtext>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <msqrt>
                <mo fence="false" stretchy="false">
                 ⟨
                 <!-- ⟨ -->
                </mo>
                <msup>
                 <mi>
                  v
                 </mi>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mn>
                   2
                  </mn>
                 </mrow>
                </msup>
                <mo fence="false" stretchy="false">
                 ⟩
                 <!-- ⟩ -->
                </mo>
               </msqrt>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <msqrt>
                <mfrac>
                 <mrow>
                  <mn>
                   3
                  </mn>
                  <msub>
                   <mi>
                    k
                   </mi>
                   <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                    <mi>
                     B
                    </mi>
                   </mrow>
                  </msub>
                  <mi>
                   T
                  </mi>
                 </mrow>
                 <mi>
                  m
                 </mi>
                </mfrac>
               </msqrt>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <msqrt>
                <mfrac>
                 <mrow>
                  <mn>
                   3
                  </mn>
                  <mi>
                   R
                  </mi>
                  <mi>
                   T
                  </mi>
                 </mrow>
                 <mi>
                  M
                 </mi>
                </mfrac>
               </msqrt>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ,
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle v_{\text{rms}}={\sqrt {\langle v^{2}\rangle }}={\sqrt {\frac {3k_{B}T}{m}}}={\sqrt {\frac {3RT}{M}}},}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="v_{{{\text{rms}}}}={\sqrt  {\langle v^{2}\rangle }}={\sqrt  {{\frac  {3k_{B}T}{m}}}}={\sqrt  {{\frac  {3RT}{M}}}}," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/dcd9f8d37c90366232aab16f93956c6368c07dcb" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:36.65ex; height:6.176ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where
       <i>
        M
       </i>
       =
       <i>
        N
       </i>
       <sub>
        A
       </sub>
       <i>
        m
       </i>
       is the mass of a mole of gas particles. This result is useful for many applications such as
       <a href="/wiki/Graham%27s_law" title="Graham's law">
        Graham's law
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Effusion" title="Effusion">
        effusion
       </a>
       , which provides a method for
       <a href="/wiki/Enriched_uranium" title="Enriched uranium">
        enriching
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">
        uranium
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Rotational_energy_and_molecular_tumbling_in_solution">
        Rotational energy and molecular tumbling in solution
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Equipartition_theorem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Rotational energy and molecular tumbling in solution">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Angular_velocity" title="Angular velocity">
        Angular velocity
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Rotational_diffusion" title="Rotational diffusion">
        Rotational diffusion
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       A similar example is provided by a rotating molecule with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Principal_moments_of_inertia" title="Principal moments of inertia">
        principal moments of inertia
       </a>
       <i>
        I
       </i>
       <sub>
        1
       </sub>
       ,
       <i>
        I
       </i>
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       and
       <i>
        I
       </i>
       <sub>
        3
       </sub>
       . The rotational energy of such a molecule is given by
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle H_{\mathrm {rot} }={\tfrac {1}{2}}(I_{1}\omega _{1}^{2}+I_{2}\omega _{2}^{2}+I_{3}\omega _{3}^{2}),}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <msub>
               <mi>
                H
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                 <mi mathvariant="normal">
                  r
                 </mi>
                 <mi mathvariant="normal">
                  o
                 </mi>
                 <mi mathvariant="normal">
                  t
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
                <mfrac>
                 <mn>
                  1
                 </mn>
                 <mn>
                  2
                 </mn>
                </mfrac>
               </mstyle>
              </mrow>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               (
              </mo>
              <msub>
               <mi>
                I
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mn>
                 1
                </mn>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <msubsup>
               <mi>
                ω
                <!-- ω -->
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mn>
                 1
                </mn>
               </mrow>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mn>
                 2
                </mn>
               </mrow>
              </msubsup>
              <mo>
               +
              </mo>
              <msub>
               <mi>
                I
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mn>
                 2
                </mn>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <msubsup>
               <mi>
                ω
                <!-- ω -->
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mn>
                 2
                </mn>
               </mrow>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mn>
                 2
                </mn>
               </mrow>
              </msubsup>
              <mo>
               +
              </mo>
              <msub>
               <mi>
                I
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mn>
                 3
                </mn>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <msubsup>
               <mi>
                ω
                <!-- ω -->
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mn>
                 3
                </mn>
               </mrow>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mn>
                 2
                </mn>
               </mrow>
              </msubsup>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               )
              </mo>
              <mo>
               ,
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle H_{\mathrm {rot} }={\tfrac {1}{2}}(I_{1}\omega _{1}^{2}+I_{2}\omega _{2}^{2}+I_{3}\omega _{3}^{2}),}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="H_{{{\mathrm  {rot}}}}={\tfrac  {1}{2}}(I_{{1}}\omega _{{1}}^{{2}}+I_{{2}}\omega _{{2}}^{{2}}+I_{{3}}\omega _{{3}}^{{2}})," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/51812413cc38617c1799e76697653cc6c5d726d1" style="vertical-align: -1.171ex; width:30.895ex; height:3.509ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where
       <i>
        ω
       </i>
       <sub>
        1
       </sub>
       ,
       <i>
        ω
       </i>
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       , and
       <i>
        ω
       </i>
       <sub>
        3
       </sub>
       are the principal components of the
       <a href="/wiki/Angular_velocity" title="Angular velocity">
        angular velocity
       </a>
       . By exactly the same reasoning as in the translational case, equipartition implies that in thermal equilibrium the average rotational energy of each particle is (3/2)
       <i>
        k
       </i>
       <sub>
        B
       </sub>
       <i>
        T
       </i>
       . Similarly, the equipartition theorem allows the average (more precisely, the root mean square) angular speed of the molecules to be calculated.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pathria_1972_5-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-pathria_1972-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The tumbling of rigid molecules—that is, the random rotations of molecules in solution—plays a key role in the
       <a href="/wiki/Relaxation_(NMR)" title="Relaxation (NMR)">
        relaxations
       </a>
       observed by
       <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_magnetic_resonance" title="Nuclear magnetic resonance">
        nuclear magnetic resonance
       </a>
       , particularly
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protein_nuclear_magnetic_resonance_spectroscopy" title="Protein nuclear magnetic resonance spectroscopy">
        protein NMR
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Residual_dipolar_coupling" title="Residual dipolar coupling">
        residual dipolar couplings
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Rotational diffusion can also be observed by other biophysical probes such as
       <a href="/wiki/Fluorescence_anisotropy" title="Fluorescence anisotropy">
        fluorescence anisotropy
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Flow_birefringence" title="Flow birefringence">
        flow birefringence
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Dielectric_spectroscopy" title="Dielectric spectroscopy">
        dielectric spectroscopy
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Potential_energy_and_harmonic_oscillators">
        Potential energy and harmonic oscillators
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Equipartition_theorem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Potential energy and harmonic oscillators">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Equipartition applies to
       <a href="/wiki/Potential_energy" title="Potential energy">
        potential energies
       </a>
       as well as kinetic energies: important examples include
       <a href="/wiki/Harmonic_oscillator" title="Harmonic oscillator">
        harmonic oscillators
       </a>
       such as a
       <a href="/wiki/Spring_(device)" title="Spring (device)">
        spring
       </a>
       , which has a quadratic potential energy
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle H_{\text{pot}}={\tfrac {1}{2}}aq^{2},\,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <msub>
               <mi>
                H
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mtext>
                 pot
                </mtext>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
                <mfrac>
                 <mn>
                  1
                 </mn>
                 <mn>
                  2
                 </mn>
                </mfrac>
               </mstyle>
              </mrow>
              <mi>
               a
              </mi>
              <msup>
               <mi>
                q
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mn>
                 2
                </mn>
               </mrow>
              </msup>
              <mo>
               ,
              </mo>
              <mspace width="thinmathspace">
              </mspace>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle H_{\text{pot}}={\tfrac {1}{2}}aq^{2},\,}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="H_{{{\text{pot}}}}={\tfrac  12}aq^{2},\," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/305e30fc6263f69133a8df42992edf0b389f18cf" style="vertical-align: -1.171ex; width:13.693ex; height:3.509ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where the constant
       <i>
        a
       </i>
       describes the stiffness of the spring and
       <i>
        q
       </i>
       is the deviation from equilibrium. If such a one-dimensional system has mass
       <i>
        m
       </i>
       , then its kinetic energy
       <i>
        H
       </i>
       <sub>
        kin
       </sub>
       is
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle H_{\text{kin}}={\frac {1}{2}}mv^{2}={\frac {p^{2}}{2m}},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <msub>
               <mi>
                H
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mtext>
                 kin
                </mtext>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <mn>
                 1
                </mn>
                <mn>
                 2
                </mn>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mi>
               m
              </mi>
              <msup>
               <mi>
                v
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mn>
                 2
                </mn>
               </mrow>
              </msup>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <msup>
                 <mi>
                  p
                 </mi>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mn>
                   2
                  </mn>
                 </mrow>
                </msup>
                <mrow>
                 <mn>
                  2
                 </mn>
                 <mi>
                  m
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ,
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle H_{\text{kin}}={\frac {1}{2}}mv^{2}={\frac {p^{2}}{2m}},}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="H_{{{\text{kin}}}}={\frac  {1}{2}}mv^{2}={\frac  {p^{2}}{2m}}," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e8369643a22658001ce296d513792aeb9015f02c" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:21.506ex; height:5.676ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where
       <i>
        v
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        p
       </i>
       =
       <i>
        mv
       </i>
       denote the velocity and momentum of the oscillator. Combining these terms yields the total energy
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goldstein_1980_8-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-goldstein_1980-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle H=H_{\text{kin}}+H_{\text{pot}}={\frac {p^{2}}{2m}}+{\frac {1}{2}}aq^{2}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mi>
               H
              </mi>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <msub>
               <mi>
                H
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mtext>
                 kin
                </mtext>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo>
               +
              </mo>
              <msub>
               <mi>
                H
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mtext>
                 pot
                </mtext>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <msup>
                 <mi>
                  p
                 </mi>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mn>
                   2
                  </mn>
                 </mrow>
                </msup>
                <mrow>
                 <mn>
                  2
                 </mn>
                 <mi>
                  m
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               +
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <mn>
                 1
                </mn>
                <mn>
                 2
                </mn>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mi>
               a
              </mi>
              <msup>
               <mi>
                q
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mn>
                 2
                </mn>
               </mrow>
              </msup>
              <mo>
               .
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle H=H_{\text{kin}}+H_{\text{pot}}={\frac {p^{2}}{2m}}+{\frac {1}{2}}aq^{2}.}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="H=H_{{{\text{kin}}}}+H_{{{\text{pot}}}}={\frac  {p^{2}}{2m}}+{\frac  {1}{2}}aq^{2}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/288f825adacb0d21b05419cf4d3ec433fc5692aa" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:32.931ex; height:5.676ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Equipartition therefore implies that in thermal equilibrium, the oscillator has average energy
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle \langle H\rangle =\langle H_{\text{kin}}\rangle +\langle H_{\text{pot}}\rangle ={\tfrac {1}{2}}k_{B}T+{\tfrac {1}{2}}k_{B}T=k_{B}T,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mo fence="false" stretchy="false">
               ⟨
               <!-- ⟨ -->
              </mo>
              <mi>
               H
              </mi>
              <mo fence="false" stretchy="false">
               ⟩
               <!-- ⟩ -->
              </mo>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mo fence="false" stretchy="false">
               ⟨
               <!-- ⟨ -->
              </mo>
              <msub>
               <mi>
                H
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mtext>
                 kin
                </mtext>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo fence="false" stretchy="false">
               ⟩
               <!-- ⟩ -->
              </mo>
              <mo>
               +
              </mo>
              <mo fence="false" stretchy="false">
               ⟨
               <!-- ⟨ -->
              </mo>
              <msub>
               <mi>
                H
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mtext>
                 pot
                </mtext>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo fence="false" stretchy="false">
               ⟩
               <!-- ⟩ -->
              </mo>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
                <mfrac>
                 <mn>
                  1
                 </mn>
                 <mn>
                  2
                 </mn>
                </mfrac>
               </mstyle>
              </mrow>
              <msub>
               <mi>
                k
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>
                 B
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mi>
               T
              </mi>
              <mo>
               +
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
                <mfrac>
                 <mn>
                  1
                 </mn>
                 <mn>
                  2
                 </mn>
                </mfrac>
               </mstyle>
              </mrow>
              <msub>
               <mi>
                k
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>
                 B
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mi>
               T
              </mi>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <msub>
               <mi>
                k
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>
                 B
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mi>
               T
              </mi>
              <mo>
               ,
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle \langle H\rangle =\langle H_{\text{kin}}\rangle +\langle H_{\text{pot}}\rangle ={\tfrac {1}{2}}k_{B}T+{\tfrac {1}{2}}k_{B}T=k_{B}T,}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="\langle H\rangle =\langle H_{{{\text{kin}}}}\rangle +\langle H_{{{\text{pot}}}}\rangle ={\tfrac  {1}{2}}k_{B}T+{\tfrac  {1}{2}}k_{B}T=k_{B}T," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a8cd4056f9de06e6e362eaf8b517f1cbede690d9" style="vertical-align: -1.171ex; width:48.354ex; height:3.509ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where the angular brackets
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle \left\langle \ldots \right\rangle }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mrow>
              <mo>
               ⟨
              </mo>
              <mo>
               …
               <!-- … -->
              </mo>
              <mo>
               ⟩
              </mo>
             </mrow>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle \left\langle \ldots \right\rangle }
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="\left\langle \ldots \right\rangle " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/977d09845a19bebb1af784016a4008a2ef3f5e88" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:4.533ex; height:2.843ex;"/>
       </span>
       denote the average of the enclosed quantity,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huang_1987_9-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-huang_1987-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       This result is valid for any type of harmonic oscillator, such as a
       <a href="/wiki/Pendulum" title="Pendulum">
        pendulum
       </a>
       , a vibrating molecule or a passive
       <a href="/wiki/Electronic_oscillator" title="Electronic oscillator">
        electronic oscillator
       </a>
       . Systems of such oscillators arise in many situations; by equipartition, each such oscillator receives an average total energy
       <i>
        k
       </i>
       <sub>
        B
       </sub>
       <i>
        T
       </i>
       and hence contributes
       <i>
        k
       </i>
       <sub>
        B
       </sub>
       to the system's
       <a href="/wiki/Heat_capacity" title="Heat capacity">
        heat capacity
       </a>
       . This can be used to derive the formula for
       <a href="/wiki/Johnson%E2%80%93Nyquist_noise" title="Johnson–Nyquist noise">
        Johnson–Nyquist noise
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mandl_1971_10-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-mandl_1971-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Dulong%E2%80%93Petit_law" title="Dulong–Petit law">
        Dulong–Petit law
       </a>
       of solid heat capacities. The latter application was particularly significant in the history of equipartition.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:300px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:V%C3%A4tskefas.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="257" data-file-width="298" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ed/V%C3%A4tskefas.png" width="298"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         Figure 3. Atoms in a crystal can vibrate about their equilibrium positions in the
         <a href="/wiki/Crystal_structure" title="Crystal structure">
          lattice
         </a>
         . Such vibrations account largely for the
         <a href="/wiki/Heat_capacity" title="Heat capacity">
          heat capacity
         </a>
         of crystalline
         <a href="/wiki/Dielectric" title="Dielectric">
          dielectrics
         </a>
         ; with
         <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">
          metals
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">
          electrons
         </a>
         also contribute to the heat capacity.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Specific_heat_capacity_of_solids">
        Specific heat capacity of solids
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Equipartition_theorem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Specific heat capacity of solids">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <dl>
         <dd>
          <i>
           For more details on the molar specific heat capacities of
           <a href="/wiki/Solid" title="Solid">
            solids
           </a>
           , see
           <a href="/wiki/Einstein_solid" title="Einstein solid">
            Einstein solid
           </a>
           and
           <a href="/wiki/Debye_model" title="Debye model">
            Debye model
           </a>
           .
          </i>
         </dd>
        </dl>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       An important application of the equipartition theorem is to the specific heat capacity of a crystalline solid. Each atom in such a solid can oscillate in three independent directions, so the solid can be viewed as a system of 3
       <i>
        N
       </i>
       independent
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simple_harmonic_oscillator" title="Simple harmonic oscillator">
        simple harmonic oscillators
       </a>
       , where
       <i>
        N
       </i>
       denotes the number of atoms in the lattice. Since each harmonic oscillator has average energy
       <i>
        k
       </i>
       <sub>
        B
       </sub>
       <i>
        T
       </i>
       , the average total energy of the solid is 3
       <i>
        Nk
       </i>
       <sub>
        B
       </sub>
       <i>
        T
       </i>
       , and its heat capacity is 3
       <i>
        Nk
       </i>
       <sub>
        B
       </sub>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       By taking
       <i>
        N
       </i>
       to be the
       <a href="/wiki/Avogadro_constant" title="Avogadro constant">
        Avogadro constant
       </a>
       <i>
        N
       </i>
       <sub>
        A
       </sub>
       , and using the relation
       <i>
        R
       </i>
       =
       <i>
        N
       </i>
       <sub>
        A
       </sub>
       <i>
        k
       </i>
       <sub>
        B
       </sub>
       between the
       <a href="/wiki/Gas_constant" title="Gas constant">
        gas constant
       </a>
       <i>
        R
       </i>
       and the Boltzmann constant
       <i>
        k
       </i>
       <sub>
        B
       </sub>
       , this provides an explanation for the
       <a href="/wiki/Dulong%E2%80%93Petit_law" title="Dulong–Petit law">
        Dulong–Petit law
       </a>
       of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Specific_heat_capacity" title="Specific heat capacity">
        specific heat capacities
       </a>
       of solids, which stated that the specific heat capacity (per unit mass) of a solid element is inversely proportional to its
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_weight" title="Atomic weight">
        atomic weight
       </a>
       . A modern version is that the molar heat capacity of a solid is
       <i>
        3R
       </i>
       ≈ 6 cal/(mol·K).
      </p>
      <p>
       However, this law is inaccurate at lower temperatures, due to quantum effects; it is also inconsistent with the experimentally derived
       <a href="/wiki/Third_law_of_thermodynamics" title="Third law of thermodynamics">
        third law of thermodynamics
       </a>
       , according to which the molar heat capacity of any substance must go to zero as the temperature goes to absolute zero.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mandl_1971_10-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-mandl_1971-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A more accurate theory, incorporating quantum effects, was developed by
       <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">
        Albert Einstein
       </a>
       (1907) and
       <a href="/wiki/Peter_Debye" title="Peter Debye">
        Peter Debye
       </a>
       (1911).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pais_1982_11-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-pais_1982-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Many other physical systems can be modeled as sets of
       <a href="/wiki/Oscillation#Coupled_oscillations" title="Oscillation">
        coupled oscillators
       </a>
       . The motions of such oscillators can be decomposed into
       <a href="/wiki/Normal_mode" title="Normal mode">
        normal modes
       </a>
       , like the vibrational modes of a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Piano_string" title="Piano string">
        piano string
       </a>
       or the
       <a href="/wiki/Resonance" title="Resonance">
        resonances
       </a>
       of an
       <a href="/wiki/Organ_pipe" title="Organ pipe">
        organ pipe
       </a>
       . On the other hand, equipartition often breaks down for such systems, because there is no exchange of energy between the normal modes. In an extreme situation, the modes are independent and so their energies are independently conserved. This shows that some sort of mixing of energies, formally called
       <i>
        ergodicity
       </i>
       , is important for the law of equipartition to hold.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Sedimentation_of_particles">
        Sedimentation of particles
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Equipartition_theorem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Sedimentation of particles">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Sedimentation" title="Sedimentation">
        Sedimentation
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Mason%E2%80%93Weaver_equation" title="Mason–Weaver equation">
        Mason–Weaver equation
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Brewing" title="Brewing">
        Brewing
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Potential energies are not always quadratic in the position. However, the equipartition theorem also shows that if a degree of freedom
       <i>
        x
       </i>
       contributes only a multiple of
       <i>
        x
       </i>
       <sup>
        s
       </sup>
       (for a fixed real number
       <i>
        s
       </i>
       ) to the energy, then in thermal equilibrium the average energy of that part is
       <i>
        k
       </i>
       <sub>
        B
       </sub>
       <i>
        T
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        s
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       There is a simple application of this extension to the
       <a href="/wiki/Sedimentation" title="Sedimentation">
        sedimentation
       </a>
       of particles under
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitation" title="Gravitation">
        gravity
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tolman_1918_12-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-tolman_1918-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       For example, the haze sometimes seen in
       <a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">
        beer
       </a>
       can be caused by clumps of
       <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">
        proteins
       </a>
       that
       <a href="/wiki/Rayleigh_scattering" title="Rayleigh scattering">
        scatter
       </a>
       light.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Over time, these clumps settle downwards under the influence of gravity, causing more haze near the bottom of a bottle than near its top. However, in a process working in the opposite direction, the particles also
       <a href="/wiki/Diffusion" title="Diffusion">
        diffuse
       </a>
       back up towards the top of the bottle. Once equilibrium has been reached, the equipartition theorem may be used to determine the average position of a particular clump of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buoyant_mass" title="Buoyant mass">
        buoyant mass
       </a>
       <i>
        m
       </i>
       <sub>
        b
       </sub>
       . For an infinitely tall bottle of beer, the gravitational
       <a href="/wiki/Potential_energy" title="Potential energy">
        potential energy
       </a>
       is given by
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle H^{\mathrm {grav} }=m_{\rm {b}}gz\,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <msup>
               <mi>
                H
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                 <mi mathvariant="normal">
                  g
                 </mi>
                 <mi mathvariant="normal">
                  r
                 </mi>
                 <mi mathvariant="normal">
                  a
                 </mi>
                 <mi mathvariant="normal">
                  v
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
               </mrow>
              </msup>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <msub>
               <mi>
                m
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                 <mi mathvariant="normal">
                  b
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mi>
               g
              </mi>
              <mi>
               z
              </mi>
              <mspace width="thinmathspace">
              </mspace>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle H^{\mathrm {grav} }=m_{\rm {b}}gz\,}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="H^{{{\mathrm  {grav}}}}=m_{{{\rm {b}}}}gz\," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3b9a8ba01dfc8f08895dda7f6188f719fd421b7d" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:14.369ex; height:2.676ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where
       <i>
        z
       </i>
       is the height of the protein clump in the bottle and
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_gravity" title="Earth's gravity">
         g
        </a>
       </i>
       is the
       <a href="/wiki/Acceleration" title="Acceleration">
        acceleration
       </a>
       due to gravity. Since
       <i>
        s
       </i>
       = 1, the average potential energy of a protein clump equals
       <i>
        k
       </i>
       <sub>
        B
       </sub>
       <i>
        T
       </i>
       . Hence, a protein clump with a buoyant mass of 10
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dalton_(unit)" title="Dalton (unit)">
        MDa
       </a>
       (roughly the size of a
       <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">
        virus
       </a>
       ) would produce a haze with an average height of about 2 cm at equilibrium. The process of such sedimentation to equilibrium is described by the
       <a href="/wiki/Mason%E2%80%93Weaver_equation" title="Mason–Weaver equation">
        Mason–Weaver equation
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mason_1924_14-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-mason_1924-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Equipartition_theorem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <dl>
         <dd>
          <i>
           This article uses the non-
           <a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Units" title="International System of Units">
            SI
           </a>
           unit of
          </i>
          <a href="/wiki/Calorie" title="Calorie">
           cal
          </a>
          /(
          <a href="/wiki/Mole_(unit)" title="Mole (unit)">
           mol
          </a>
          ·
          <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">
           K
          </a>
          )
          <i>
           for heat capacity, because it offers greater accuracy for single digits.
           <br/>
           For an approximate conversion to the corresponding SI unit of
          </i>
          J/(mol·K)
          <i>
           , such values should be multiplied by 4.2
          </i>
          <a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">
           J
          </a>
          /cal.
         </dd>
        </dl>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       The equipartition of kinetic energy was proposed initially in 1843, and more correctly in 1845, by
       <a href="/wiki/John_James_Waterston" title="John James Waterston">
        John James Waterston
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1859,
       <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">
        James Clerk Maxwell
       </a>
       argued that the kinetic heat energy of a gas is equally divided between linear and rotational energy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1876,
       <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Boltzmann" title="Ludwig Boltzmann">
        Ludwig Boltzmann
       </a>
       expanded on this principle by showing that the average energy was divided equally among all the independent components of motion in a system.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Boltzmann applied the equipartition theorem to provide a theoretical explanation of the
       <a href="/wiki/Dulong%E2%80%93Petit_law" title="Dulong–Petit law">
        Dulong–Petit law
       </a>
       for the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Specific_heat" title="Specific heat">
        specific heat capacities
       </a>
       of solids.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DiatomicSpecHeat1.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1799" data-file-width="2398" height="338" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/DiatomicSpecHeat1.png/450px-DiatomicSpecHeat1.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/DiatomicSpecHeat1.png/675px-DiatomicSpecHeat1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/DiatomicSpecHeat1.png/900px-DiatomicSpecHeat1.png 2x" width="450"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DiatomicSpecHeat1.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Figure 4. Idealized plot of the
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Specific_heat_capacity" title="Specific heat capacity">
          molar specific heat
         </a>
         of a diatomic gas against temperature. It agrees with the value (7/2)
         <i>
          R
         </i>
         predicted by equipartition at high temperatures (where
         <i>
          R
         </i>
         is the
         <a href="/wiki/Gas_constant" title="Gas constant">
          gas constant
         </a>
         ), but decreases to (5/2)
         <i>
          R
         </i>
         and then (3/2)
         <i>
          R
         </i>
         at lower temperatures, as the vibrational and rotational modes of motion are "frozen out". The failure of the equipartition theorem led to a paradox that was only resolved by
         <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">
          quantum mechanics
         </a>
         . For most molecules, the transitional temperature T
         <sub>
          rot
         </sub>
         is much less than room temperature, whereas
         <i>
          T
         </i>
         <sub>
          vib
         </sub>
         can be ten times larger or more. A typical example is
         <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">
          carbon monoxide
         </a>
         , CO, for which
         <i>
          T
         </i>
         <sub>
          rot
         </sub>
         ≈ 2.8
         <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">
          K
         </a>
         and
         <i>
          T
         </i>
         <sub>
          vib
         </sub>
         ≈ 3103
         <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">
          K
         </a>
         . For molecules with very large or weakly bound atoms,
         <i>
          T
         </i>
         <sub>
          vib
         </sub>
         can be close to room temperature (about 300 K); for example,
         <i>
          T
         </i>
         <sub>
          vib
         </sub>
         ≈ 308 K for
         <a href="/wiki/Iodine" title="Iodine">
          iodine
         </a>
         gas, I
         <sub>
          2
         </sub>
         .
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcquarrie_2000c_19-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-mcquarrie_2000c-19">
           [19]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The history of the equipartition theorem is intertwined with that of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Specific_heat_capacity" title="Specific heat capacity">
        specific heat capacity
       </a>
       , both of which were studied in the 19th century. In 1819, the French physicists
       <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Louis_Dulong" title="Pierre Louis Dulong">
        Pierre Louis Dulong
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Alexis_Th%C3%A9r%C3%A8se_Petit" title="Alexis Thérèse Petit">
        Alexis Thérèse Petit
       </a>
       discovered that the specific heat capacities of solid elements at room temperature were inversely proportional to the atomic weight of the element.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Their law was used for many years as a technique for measuring atomic weights.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pais_1982_11-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-pais_1982-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, subsequent studies by
       <a href="/wiki/James_Dewar" title="James Dewar">
        James Dewar
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Friedrich_Weber" title="Heinrich Friedrich Weber">
        Heinrich Friedrich Weber
       </a>
       showed that this
       <a href="/wiki/Dulong%E2%80%93Petit_law" title="Dulong–Petit law">
        Dulong–Petit law
       </a>
       holds only at high
       <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">
        temperatures
       </a>
       ;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       at lower temperatures, or for exceptionally hard solids such as
       <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">
        diamond
       </a>
       , the specific heat capacity was lower.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Experimental observations of the specific heat capacities of gases also raised concerns about the validity of the equipartition theorem. The theorem predicts that the molar heat capacity of simple monatomic gases should be roughly 3 cal/(mol·K), whereas that of diatomic gases should be roughly 7 cal/(mol·K). Experiments confirmed the former prediction,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kundt_1876_3-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-kundt_1876-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but found that molar heat capacities of diatomic gases were typically about 5 cal/(mol·K),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wueller_1896_23-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Wueller_1896-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and fell to about 3 cal/(mol·K) at very low temperatures.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eucken_1912_24-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Eucken_1912-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">
        Maxwell
       </a>
       noted in 1875 that the disagreement between experiment and the equipartition theorem was much worse than even these numbers suggest;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-maxwell_1875_25-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-maxwell_1875-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       since atoms have internal parts, heat energy should go into the motion of these internal parts, making the predicted specific heats of monatomic and diatomic gases much higher than 3 cal/(mol·K) and 7 cal/(mol·K), respectively.
      </p>
      <p>
       A third discrepancy concerned the specific heat of metals.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kittel_1996_26-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-kittel_1996-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       According to the classical
       <a href="/wiki/Drude_model" title="Drude model">
        Drude model
       </a>
       , metallic electrons act as a nearly ideal gas, and so they should contribute (3/2)
       <i>
        N
       </i>
       <sub>
        e
       </sub>
       <i>
        k
       </i>
       <sub>
        B
       </sub>
       to the heat capacity by the equipartition theorem, where
       <i>
        N
       </i>
       <sub>
        e
       </sub>
       is the number of electrons. Experimentally, however, electrons contribute little to the heat capacity: the molar heat capacities of many conductors and insulators are nearly the same.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kittel_1996_26-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-kittel_1996-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Several explanations of equipartition's failure to account for molar heat capacities were proposed.
       <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Boltzmann" title="Ludwig Boltzmann">
        Boltzmann
       </a>
