      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Limitations">
        Limitations
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Equipartition_theorem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Limitations">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1D_normal_modes_(280_kB).gif">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="275" data-file-width="275" height="275" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9b/1D_normal_modes_%28280_kB%29.gif" width="275"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         Figure 9. Energy is
         <i>
          not
         </i>
         shared among the various
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Normal_modes" title="Normal modes">
          normal modes
         </a>
         in an isolated system of ideal coupled
         <a href="/wiki/Harmonic_oscillator" title="Harmonic oscillator">
          oscillators
         </a>
         ; the energy in each mode is constant and independent of the energy in the other modes. Hence, the equipartition theorem does
         <i>
          not
         </i>
         hold for such a system in the
         <a href="/wiki/Microcanonical_ensemble" title="Microcanonical ensemble">
          microcanonical ensemble
         </a>
         (when isolated), although it does hold in the
         <a href="/wiki/Canonical_ensemble" title="Canonical ensemble">
          canonical ensemble
         </a>
         (when coupled to a heat bath). However, by adding a sufficiently strong nonlinear coupling between the modes, energy will be shared and equipartition holds in both ensembles.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Requirement_of_ergodicity">
        Requirement of ergodicity
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Equipartition_theorem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Requirement of ergodicity">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Ergodicity" title="Ergodicity">
        Ergodicity
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Chaos_theory" title="Chaos theory">
        Chaos theory
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Kolmogorov%E2%80%93Arnold%E2%80%93Moser_theorem" title="Kolmogorov–Arnold–Moser theorem">
        Kolmogorov–Arnold–Moser theorem
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solitons" title="Solitons">
        Solitons
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The law of equipartition holds only for
       <a href="/wiki/Ergodic_hypothesis" title="Ergodic hypothesis">
        ergodic
       </a>
       systems in
       <a href="/wiki/Thermal_equilibrium" title="Thermal equilibrium">
        thermal equilibrium
       </a>
       , which implies that all states with the same energy must be equally likely to be populated.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huang_1987_9-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-huang_1987-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Consequently, it must be possible to exchange energy among all its various forms within the system, or with an external
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heat_bath" title="Heat bath">
        heat bath
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Canonical_ensemble" title="Canonical ensemble">
        canonical ensemble
       </a>
       . The number of physical systems that have been rigorously proven to be ergodic is small; a famous example is the
       <a href="/wiki/Dynamical_billiards" title="Dynamical billiards">
        hard-sphere system
       </a>
       of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yakov_G._Sinai" title="Yakov G. Sinai">
        Yakov Sinai
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The requirements for isolated systems to ensure
       <a href="/wiki/Ergodic_theory" title="Ergodic theory">
        ergodicity
       </a>
       —and, thus equipartition—have been studied, and provided motivation for the modern
       <a href="/wiki/Chaos_theory" title="Chaos theory">
        chaos theory
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Dynamical_system" title="Dynamical system">
        dynamical systems
       </a>
       . A chaotic
       <a href="/wiki/Hamiltonian_system" title="Hamiltonian system">
        Hamiltonian system
       </a>
       need not be ergodic, although that is usually a good assumption.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reichl_1998_49-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-reichl_1998-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A commonly cited counter-example where energy is
       <i>
        not
       </i>
       shared among its various forms and where equipartition does
       <i>
        not
       </i>
       hold in the microcanonical ensemble is a system of coupled harmonic oscillators.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reichl_1998_49-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-reichl_1998-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       If the system is isolated from the rest of the world, the energy in each
       <a href="/wiki/Normal_mode" title="Normal mode">
        normal mode
       </a>
       is constant; energy is not transferred from one mode to another. Hence, equipartition does not hold for such a system; the amount of energy in each normal mode is fixed at its initial value. If sufficiently strong nonlinear terms are present in the
       <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">
        energy
       </a>
       function, energy may be transferred between the normal modes, leading to ergodicity and rendering the law of equipartition valid. However, the
       <a href="/wiki/Kolmogorov%E2%80%93Arnold%E2%80%93Moser_theorem" title="Kolmogorov–Arnold–Moser theorem">
        Kolmogorov–Arnold–Moser theorem
       </a>
       states that energy will not be exchanged unless the nonlinear perturbations are strong enough; if they are too small, the energy will remain trapped in at least some of the modes.
      </p>
      <p>
       Another way ergodicity can be broken is by the existence of nonlinear
       <a href="/wiki/Soliton" title="Soliton">
        soliton
       </a>
       symmetries. In 1953,
       <a href="/wiki/Enrico_Fermi" title="Enrico Fermi">
        Fermi
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/John_Pasta" title="John Pasta">
        Pasta
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Stanislaw_Ulam" title="Stanislaw Ulam">
        Ulam
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Mary_Tsingou" title="Mary Tsingou">
        Tsingou
       </a>
       conducted
       <a href="/wiki/Fermi%E2%80%93Pasta%E2%80%93Ulam%E2%80%93Tsingou_problem" title="Fermi–Pasta–Ulam–Tsingou problem">
        computer simulations
       </a>
       of a vibrating string that included a non-linear term (quadratic in one test, cubic in another, and a piecewise linear approximation to a cubic in a third). They found that the behavior of the system was quite different from what intuition based on equipartition would have led them to expect. Instead of the energies in the modes becoming equally shared, the system exhibited a very complicated quasi-periodic behavior. This puzzling result was eventually explained by Kruskal and Zabusky in 1965 in a paper which, by connecting the simulated system to the
       <a href="/wiki/Korteweg%E2%80%93de_Vries_equation" title="Korteweg–de Vries equation">
        Korteweg–de Vries equation
       </a>
       led to the development of soliton mathematics.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Failure_due_to_quantum_effects">
        Failure due to quantum effects
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Equipartition_theorem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Failure due to quantum effects">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet_catastrophe" title="Ultraviolet catastrophe">
        Ultraviolet catastrophe
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_quantum_mechanics" title="History of quantum mechanics">
        History of quantum mechanics
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Identical_particles" title="Identical particles">
        Identical particles
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The law of equipartition breaks down when the thermal energy
       <i>
        k
        <sub>
         B
        </sub>
        T
       </i>
       is significantly smaller than the spacing between energy levels. Equipartition no longer holds because it is a poor approximation to assume that the energy levels form a smooth
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Continuum_(theory)" title="Continuum (theory)">
        continuum
       </a>
       , which is required in the
       <a href="#Derivations">
        derivations of the equipartition theorem above
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pathria_1972_5-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-pathria_1972-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huang_1987_9-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-huang_1987-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Historically, the failures of the classical equipartition theorem to explain
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Specific_heats" title="Specific heats">
        specific heats
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blackbody_radiation" title="Blackbody radiation">
        blackbody radiation
       </a>
       were critical in showing the need for a new theory of matter and radiation, namely,
       <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">
        quantum mechanics
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory" title="Quantum field theory">
        quantum field theory
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pais_1982_11-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-pais_1982-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Et_fig2.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="505" data-file-width="527" height="383" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Et_fig2.png/400px-Et_fig2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/57/Et_fig2.png 1.5x" width="400"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Et_fig2.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Figure 10. Log–log plot of the average energy of a quantum mechanical oscillator (shown in red) as a function of temperature. For comparison, the value predicted by the equipartition theorem is shown in black. At high temperatures, the two agree nearly perfectly, but at low temperatures when
         <i>
          k
          <sub>
           B
          </sub>
          T &lt;&lt; hν
         </i>
         , the quantum mechanical value decreases much more rapidly. This resolves the problem of the
         <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet_catastrophe" title="Ultraviolet catastrophe">
          ultraviolet catastrophe
         </a>
         : for a given temperature, the energy in the high-frequency modes (where
         <i>
          hν &gt;&gt; k
          <sub>
           B
          </sub>
          T
         </i>
         ) is almost zero.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       To illustrate the breakdown of equipartition, consider the average energy in a single (quantum) harmonic oscillator, which was discussed above for the classical case. Neglecting the irrelevant
       <a href="/wiki/Zero-point_energy" title="Zero-point energy">
        zero-point energy
       </a>
       term, its quantum energy levels are given by
       <i>
        E
        <sub>
         n
        </sub>
        = nhν
       </i>
       , where
       <i>
        h
       </i>
       is the
       <a href="/wiki/Planck_constant" title="Planck constant">
        Planck constant
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        ν
       </i>
       is the
       <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_frequency" title="Fundamental frequency">
        fundamental frequency
       </a>
       of the oscillator, and
       <i>
        n
       </i>
       is an integer. The probability of a given energy level being populated in the
       <a href="/wiki/Canonical_ensemble" title="Canonical ensemble">
        canonical ensemble
       </a>
       is given by its
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boltzmann_factor" title="Boltzmann factor">
        Boltzmann factor
       </a>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">

      <p>
       At high temperatures, when the thermal energy
       <i>
        k
       </i>
       <sub>
        B
       </sub>
       <i>
        T
       </i>
       is much greater than the spacing
       <i>
        hν
       </i>
       between energy levels, the exponential argument
       <i>
        βhν
       </i>
       is much less than one and the average energy becomes
       <i>
        k
       </i>
       <sub>
        B
       </sub>
       <i>
        T
       </i>
       , in agreement with the equipartition theorem (Figure 10). However, at low temperatures, when
       <i>
        hν
       </i>
       &gt;&gt;
       <i>
        k
       </i>
       <sub>
        B
       </sub>
       <i>
        T
       </i>
       , the average energy goes to zero—the higher-frequency energy levels are "frozen out" (Figure 10). As another example, the internal excited electronic states of a hydrogen atom do not contribute to its specific heat as a gas at room temperature, since the thermal energy
       <i>
        k
       </i>
       <sub>
        B
       </sub>
       <i>
        T
       </i>
       (roughly 0.025
       <a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">
        eV
       </a>
       ) is much smaller than the spacing between the lowest and next higher electronic energy levels (roughly 10 eV).
      </p>
      <p>
       Similar considerations apply whenever the energy level spacing is much larger than the thermal energy. For example, this reasoning was used by
       <a href="/wiki/Max_Planck" title="Max Planck">
        Max Planck
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">
        Albert Einstein
       </a>
       to resolve the
       <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet_catastrophe" title="Ultraviolet catastrophe">
        ultraviolet catastrophe
       </a>
       of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blackbody_radiation" title="Blackbody radiation">
        blackbody radiation
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Einstein1905_50-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Einstein1905-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The paradox arises because there are an infinite number of independent modes of the
       <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_field" title="Electromagnetic field">
        electromagnetic field
       </a>
       in a closed container, each of which may be treated as a harmonic oscillator. If each electromagnetic mode were to have an average energy
       <i>
        k
       </i>
       <sub>
        B
       </sub>
       <i>
        T
       </i>
       , there would be an infinite amount of energy in the container.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Einstein1905_50-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Einstein1905-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
        <a href="#cite_note-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, by the reasoning above, the average energy in the higher-frequency modes goes to zero as
       <i>
        ν
       </i>
       goes to infinity; moreover,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planck%27s_law_of_black_body_radiation" title="Planck's law of black body radiation">
        Planck's law of black body radiation
       </a>
       , which describes the experimental distribution of energy in the modes, follows from the same reasoning.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Einstein1905_50-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Einstein1905-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Other, more subtle quantum effects can lead to corrections to equipartition, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Identical_particles" title="Identical particles">
        identical particles
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Symmetry" title="Symmetry">
        continuous symmetries
       </a>
       . The effects of identical particles can be dominant at very high densities and low temperatures. For example, the
       <a href="/wiki/Valence_electron" title="Valence electron">
        valence electrons
       </a>
       in a metal can have a mean kinetic energy of a few
       <a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">
        electronvolts
       </a>
       , which would normally correspond to a temperature of tens of thousands of kelvins. Such a state, in which the density is high enough that the
       <a href="/wiki/Pauli_exclusion_principle" title="Pauli exclusion principle">
        Pauli exclusion principle
       </a>
       invalidates the classical approach, is called a
       <a href="/wiki/Degenerate_matter" title="Degenerate matter">
        degenerate fermion gas
       </a>
       . Such gases are important for the structure of
       <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">
        white dwarf
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">
        neutron stars
       </a>
       . At low temperatures, a
       <a href="/wiki/Fermionic_condensate" title="Fermionic condensate">
        fermionic analogue
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Bose%E2%80%93Einstein_condensate" title="Bose–Einstein condensate">
        Bose–Einstein condensate
       </a>
       (in which a large number of identical particles occupy the lowest-energy state) can form; such
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superfluid" title="Superfluid">
        superfluid
       </a>
       electrons are responsible for
       <a href="/wiki/Superconductivity" title="Superconductivity">
        superconductivity
       </a>
       .
      </p>

          <i>
           Papers
          </i>

          <br/>
          <cite class="citation journal">
           <a href="/wiki/John_James_Waterston" title="John James Waterston">
            Waterston, JJ
           </a>
           (1851).
           <i>

           </i>

           </b>
           : 6.
          </cite>
          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=British+Association+Reports&amp;rft.volume=21&amp;rft.pages=6&amp;rft.date=1851&amp;rft.aulast=Waterston&amp;rft.aufirst=JJ&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEquipartition+theorem">
           <span style="display:none;">
           </span>
          </span>
          <span class="error citation-comment" style="font-size:100%">
           Missing or empty
           <code style="color:inherit; border:inherit; padding:inherit;">
            |title=
           </code>
           (
           <a href="/wiki/Help:CS1_errors#citation_missing_title" title="Help:CS1 errors">
            help
           </a>
           )
          </span>
          Waterston's key paper was written and submitted in 1845 to the
          <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">
           Royal Society
          </a>
          . After refusing to publish his work, the Society also refused to return his manuscript and stored it among its files. The manuscript was discovered in 1891 by
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Strutt,_3rd_Baron_Rayleigh" title="John Strutt, 3rd Baron Rayleigh">
           Lord Rayleigh
          </a>
          , who criticized the original reviewer for failing to recognize the significance of Waterston's work. Waterston managed to publish his ideas in 1851, and therefore has priority over Maxwell for enunciating the first version of the equipartition theorem.
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-16">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-16">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <cite class="citation book">
           <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">
            Maxwell, JC
           </a>
           (2003). "Illustrations of the Dynamical Theory of Gases". In WD Niven.
           <i>
            The Scientific Papers of James Clerk Maxwell
           </i>
           . New York: Dover. Vol.1, pp. 377–409.
           <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
            ISBN
           </a>
           <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-486-49560-6" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-486-49560-6">
            978-0-486-49560-6
           </a>
           .
          </cite>
          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Illustrations+of+the+Dynamical+Theory+of+Gases&amp;rft.btitle=The+Scientific+Papers+of+James+Clerk+Maxwell&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.pages=Vol.1%2C+pp.+377-409&amp;rft.pub=Dover&amp;rft.date=2003&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-486-49560-6&amp;rft.aulast=Maxwell&amp;rft.aufirst=JC&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEquipartition+theorem">
           <span style="display:none;">
           </span>
          </span>
          Read by Prof. Maxwell at a Meeting of the British Association at Aberdeen on 21 September 1859.
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-17">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-17">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <cite class="citation journal">
           <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Boltzmann" title="Ludwig Boltzmann">
            Boltzmann, L
           </a>
           (1871). "Einige allgemeine Sätze über Wärmegleichgewicht (Some general statements on thermal equilibrium)".
           <i>
            Wiener Berichte
           </i>
           (in German).
           <b>
            63
           </b>
           : 679–711.
          </cite>
          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Wiener+Berichte&amp;rft.atitle=Einige+allgemeine+S%C3%A4tze+%C3%BCber+W%C3%A4rmegleichgewicht+%28Some+general+statements+on+thermal+equilibrium%29&amp;rft.volume=63&amp;rft.pages=679-711&amp;rft.date=1871&amp;rft.aulast=Boltzmann&amp;rft.aufirst=L&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEquipartition+theorem">
           <span style="display:none;">
           </span>
          </span>
          In this preliminary work, Boltzmann showed that the average total kinetic energy equals the average total potential energy when a system is acted upon by external harmonic forces.
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-18">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-18">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <cite class="citation journal">
           <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Boltzmann" title="Ludwig Boltzmann">
            Boltzmann, L
           </a>
           (1876). "Über die Natur der Gasmoleküle (On the nature of gas molecules)".
           <i>
            Wiener Berichte
           </i>
           (in German).
           <b>
            74
           </b>
           : 553–560.
          </cite>

          </cite>
